Applying al-Khafi: The Theft Verse and Dispute on Pickpocket and Grave-Robber
Applying al-Khafi to the theft verse with dispute on tarrar and nabbash.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of al-Khafi to the Quranic verse on theft (5:38), explain the Hanafi rulings for pickpocket (tarrar) and grave-robber (nabbash), and evaluate the opposing views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The thief verse is khafi for pickpocket (increase→cut) and grave-robber (deficiency→no cut); Hanafis rule accordingly, while Abu Yusuf and Shafi'i apply the general.

Having understood the definition of al-Khafi, we now see it in action. The verse 'Cut off their hands' (5:38) appears clear for the ordinary thief, but its application to pickpockets and grave-robbers is hidden due to external causes. By analyzing the increase or deficiency in the meaning of theft, the Hanafi school derives differentiated rulings. This lesson walks through the reasoning and the ensuing inter-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the source text and its apparent meaning
The Quranic verse (5:38) commands cutting the hand of every male and female thief. In its 'zahir' meaning, it applies to anyone called 'thief' (sariq).
Remember that al-Zahir is the default category for clear texts.
2
2. Recognize the external cause making the text khafi
In common usage (urf al-lisan), a pickpocket is called 'tarrar', not 'sariq', and a grave-robber is called 'nabbash'. This different naming is an external accidental cause that veils the literal applicability of the verse to them.
This is precisely the definition of al-Khafi: hiddenness due to an external cause.
3
3. Perform the required research (talab)
Investigate the legal meaning of 'sariq' to see if tarrar and nabbash are included. The definition: taking valuable property from a guarded place (hirz) secretly, without ambiguity, while the owner intends to guard it. Examine each case.
The research focuses on the elements of 'hirz' and the owner's intent.
4
4. Determine increase or deficiency
For tarrar: he steals from a vigilant owner by trickery—this adds an element of stealth, thus an increase in meaning over the ordinary thief. For nabbash: he steals from the dead, who do not intend to guard—this is a deficiency in the meaning of theft.
Increase leads to extension of the hadd; deficiency leads to non-extension.
5
5. State the ruling and note the dispute
Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): cut for tarrar (by dalalat al-nass), no cut for nabbash. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i argue for cutting both because the verse is general. The passage mentions that even if the grave is locked, the more correct (asahh) view is no cutting for nabbash.
The dispute hinges on whether the verse's generality overrides the khafi nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان المتقابلات للأقسام الأربعة السابقة

تعريف الخفي وحكمه فإن كلا من هؤلاء مترتب في الخفاء ترتب الأصل في الظهور، فإذا كان في الظاهر أدنى ظهور، أي أقسام الخفاء في الشدة والضعف فلابد أن يكون في الخفى أدنى خفاء، وهكذا القياس فلا ينال مراده إلا بالطلب، فصار كمن اختفى في المدينة بنوع حيلة عارضة من غير تغيير لباس وهيأة، ثم في قوله: "بعارض غير الصيغة" مسامحة، والأظهر أن يقول بعارض من غير الصيغة. كما في عبارة شمس الأئمة الحلواني، وقوله: "لا ينال إلا بالطلب" ليس قيدا احترازيا، بل بيان للواقع وتأكيد للخفاء. وحكمه النظر فيه ليعلم أن اختفاءه لمزية أو نقصان فيظهر المراد أي حكم الخفي النظر فيه وهو الطلب الأول ليعلم أن اختفاءه لأجل زيادة المعنى فيه على الظاهر أو نقصانه فيه، فحينئذ المراد أي بعد الطلب يظهر المراد، فيحكم في الزيادة على حسب ما يعلم من الظاهر، ولا يحكم في النقصان قط.

كآية السرقة في حق الطرار والنباش، فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَالسَّارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَاقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا ﴾ (المائدة: 38) ظاهر في حق وجوب قطع اليد لكل سارق خفي في حق الطرار والنباش؛ لأنهما اختصا باسم آخر غير السارق في عرف أهل اللسان، فتأملنا فوجدنا أن اختصاص الطرار باسم آخر لأجل زيادة معنى السرقة إذ السرقة هو أخذ مال محترم محرز خفية، وهو يسرق ممن هو يقظان قاصد لحفظ المال بضرب غفلة وفترة تعتريه، واختصاص النباش باسم آخر لأجل نقصان معنى السرقة فيه؛ لأنه يسرق من الموتى الذي هو غير قاصد للحفظ، فعدينا حكم القطع إلى الطرار لأجل الزيادة فيه بدلالة النص ولم نعد إلى النباش لأجل النقصان فيه، ولو كان القبر في بيت مقفل قيل: لا يقطع النباش؛ لما ذكرنا، وقيل: يقطع؛ لوجود الحرز بالمكان وإن لم يوجد بالحافظ، وهذا كله عندنا، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي : يقطع لكل سارق إلخ فحكم السارق خفي في حق الطرار والنباش لعارض من الخارج، وهو اختصاصهما باسم آخر يعرفان به (السنبلي لأنهما اختصا إلخ فتطرقت الشبهة في أنه يشملها اسم السارق أم لا، فتأملنا في المعنى الشرعي للسارق فوجدنا إلخ. (القمر) إذ السرقة إلخ : قلت : التعريف الجامع للسرقة: أخذ مال معتبر شرعًا، من حرز أجنبي لا شبهة فيه خفية، وهو قاصد للحفظ، في نومه أو غيبته احترز بالأول ما دون النصاب، وبالثاني عن الأخذ غير الحرز، وبالثالث عن ذي رحم محرم وبالرابع عن مال فيه شبهة الحرز كمال الشركة، وبالخامس عن والانتهاب، وبالسادس عن النبش وبالسابع عن الطرار. (السنبلي) :محترم أي معزز بأن يكون المال متقوما يحل الانتفاع به شرعًا، فلا قطع بسرقة خمر مسلم، وأن يكون عشرة دراهم، فلا قطع بسرقة أقل منها (القمر) محرز واحترز بقوله : محرز عن الأخذ من غير حرز، ويقول: خفية عن الانتهاب والغصب كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر) بدلالة لأن الحكم إذا ثبت في الأدنى يثبت في الأعلى بالأولى. (المحشي) بدلالة النص متعلق بقوله فعدينا وفيه أن الحد للزجر، وزاجر الأدنى لا يثبت في الأعلى دلالة، ألا ترى أن الكفارة في قتل الخطاء لا تثبت في قتل العمد دلالة على أن الزاجر مشروع فيما كثر وقوعه، فلا يلزم شرعه فيما قل وقوعه كالطر، فإنه أقل وقوعا من السرقة، ولذا قال بعض شراح أصول البزدوي: إن إثبات القطع في الطرار بالعبارة؛ لأن المطلق يتناول الكامل فلأن يتناول الأكمل أولى. (القمر)
قيل: لا يقطع إلخ: هو الأصح كذا في "الدر المختار" وهو قول الإمام السرخسي كذا قال البرجندي. (القمر) لما ذكرنا أي لأجل النقصان في اللفظ، وكل من الناس يتأول في الدخول في ذلك البيت لزيارة القبر. (القمر) : وهذا أي عدم قطع النباش عند الإمام الأعظم وعند محمد رحمها. (القمر)
Explanation of the correspondences for the previous four divisions.

Definition of al-Khafi and its ruling: For each of these is arranged in hiddenness as the original is arranged in clarity. So since in al-Zahir there is minimal clarity (i.e., the degrees of hiddenness in intensity and weakness), it must be that in al-Khafi there is minimal hiddenness, and likewise by analogy. So one does not attain its meaning except by seeking. So it becomes like one who hid in the city by a kind of temporary trick without changing clothes and appearance. Then in his statement: 'by an accidental cause other than the wording' there is looseness. The more apparent is that he should say 'by an accidental cause from other than the wording' as in the phrase of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani. And his statement: 'it is not attained except by seeking' is not a restrictive condition, but rather a clarification of the reality and a confirmation of hiddenness. And its ruling is to reflect upon it to know that its hiddenness is due to an increase or a deficiency, so the meaning appears; i.e., the ruling of al-Khafi is reflection upon it—that is the first seeking—to know that its hiddenness is because of increase of meaning over the apparent (zahir) or deficiency in it. Then the meaning—i.e., after seeking—the meaning appears. So one rules regarding the increase according to what is known from the apparent, and one never rules regarding the deficiency.

Like the verse of theft regarding the pickpocket (tarrar) and the grave-robber (nabbash). For Allah's statement: 'The male thief and the female thief, cut off their hands' (5:38) is apparent (zahir) regarding the obligation of cutting the hand for every thief, but hidden (khafi) regarding the pickpocket and grave-robber; because they are distinguished by another name besides 'thief' in the custom of the people of the language. So we reflected and found that the distinction of the pickpocket by another name is for an increase in the meaning of theft; for theft is taking property that is respected (muhtaram), guarded (muhraz), secretly, and he steals from one who is awake, intending to guard the property, by striking with heedlessness and a passing state of weakness. And the distinction of the grave-robber by another name is for a deficiency in the meaning of theft; because he steals from the dead who do not intend to guard. So we extended the ruling of cutting to the pickpocket due to the increase in it by implication of the text (dalalat al-nass), and we did not extend it to the grave-robber due to the deficiency. And if the grave is in a locked house, it is said: the grave-robber is not cut off; for what we mentioned, and it is said: he is cut off due to the existence of guarding (hirz) by place even if not by guardian. And all this is according to us (Hanafis). And Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: He is cut off for every thief... etc. So the ruling of thief is hidden with respect to the pickpocket and grave-robber due to an external accidental cause, which is their being distinguished by another name by which they are known. (al-Sunbuli: because they are distinguished etc., so doubt arises whether the name 'thief' covers them or not, so we reflected on the legal meaning of thief and found... etc.) (al-Qamar) 'For theft is...' I say: The comprehensive definition of theft: taking property that is legally considered valuable, from a guarded place (hirz), by a stranger, without ambiguity, secretly, while the owner is intending to guard, whether in sleep or absence. By the first he excludes less than the nisab, by the second he excludes taking from non-hirz, by the third he excludes a mahram relative, by the fourth he excludes property with ambiguity of hirz like partnership property, by the fifth he excludes plundering and usurpation, by the sixth he excludes grave-robbery, and by the seventh he excludes pickpocketing. (al-Sunbuli) 'Respected' i.e., valued such that the property is of value (muttaqawwim) and permissible to benefit from legally; so no cutting for stealing a Muslim's wine, and it must be ten dirhams; so no cutting for less. (al-Qamar) 'Guarded' - he guarded by his statement 'guarded' against taking from non-hirz. And he said: 'secretly' against plunder and usurpation; so said Ibn al-Malik. (al-Qamar) 'By implication' because when the ruling is established for the lower, it is established for the higher a fortiori. (al-Muhashi) 'By implication of the text' is attached to his statement 'we extended' and there is in it that the hadd is for deterrence, and the deterrence of the lower is not established for the higher by implication. Do you not see that expiation in accidental killing is not established for intentional killing by implication? That is because deterrence is legislated where occurrence is frequent, so its legislation is not necessary where occurrence is rare, like pickpocketing, for it is less frequent than theft. Hence some commentators on Usul al-Bazdawi said: The establishment of cutting for the pickpocket is by explicit wording (al-ibara); because the absolute (mutlaq) encompasses the perfect, so encompassing the more perfect is more appropriate. (al-Qamar)
'It is said: he is not cut off' etc.: That is the more correct (asahh) as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and it is the saying of Imam al-Sarakhsi; as said al-Barjandi. (al-Qamar) 'For what we mentioned' i.e., due to the deficiency in the wording, and every person has the interpretation of entering that house to visit the grave. (al-Qamar) 'And this' i.e., the non-cutting of the grave-robber according to Imam al-A'zam and according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them). (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the category of al-Khafi (the hidden) in the Hanafi hierarchy of textual clarity. It begins by defining al-Khafi: a text that is not immediately understandable but requires research (talab) to uncover its intended meaning. The author explains that al-Khafi is the opposite of al-Zahir (the apparent); just as al-Zahir has the minimum degree of clarity, al-Khafi has the minimum degree of hiddenness. The hiddenness arises from an external cause (arid) other than the wording itself. The ruling (hukm) for al-Khafi is that the mujtahid must investigate it to determine whether the hiddenness indicates an increase in meaning over the apparent or a deficiency. If it indicates an increase, the ruling is extended accordingly; if it indicates a deficiency, the ruling is not applied. This principle is then applied to the Quranic verse on theft (5:38). The verse is zahir for the ordinary thief, but khafi for the pickpocket (tarrar) and grave-robber (nabbash) because they have distinct names. Through research, the Hanafis conclude that the pickpocket steals from a vigilant person, thus his act has an additional element (increase), so he is subject to the hadd of cutting. The grave-robber steals from the dead, who have no intent to guard, thus his act is deficient in the meaning of theft, so he is not subject to cutting. This is the Hanafi position (Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i disagree and hold that both should be cut because the verse is general. The passage also discusses the condition of hirz (guardedness) and the dispute about cutting the grave-robber if the grave is in a locked house. The more correct view (asahh) according to later Hanafi authorities like al-Sarakhsi is non-cutting. This lesson demonstrates how the usul concept of al-Khafi is concretely applied in fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
الخفي (al-Khafi): A text whose meaning is not immediately clear due to an external accidental cause, requiring research to uncover whether the hiddenness indicates increase or deficiency in meaning compared to the apparent.
الطرار (al-Tarrar): A pickpocket; one who steals from a person who is awake by distracting them or taking advantage of a moment of heedlessness.
النباش (al-Nabbash): A grave-robber; one who steals property from a dead body in a grave.
الحزر (al-Hirz): A guarded place; the secure location from which theft occurs, which is a condition for the hadd of cutting.
بدلالة النص (bi dalalat al-nass): By implication of the text; a method of legal derivation where the ruling is extended from a stated case to an unstated case because the unstated case is understood from the wording or context as being more deserving.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling for a grave-robber (nabbash) who steals from an unlocked grave?
A) His hand is cut because the verse is general.
B) His hand is cut because the grave is a guarded place.
C) His hand is not cut due to deficiency in the meaning of theft (the dead do not intend to guard).
D) His hand is cut only if the grave is in a locked house.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis cut the pickpocket but not the grave-robber because of the increase/deficiency of meaning discovered through research on the khafi text.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy
intermediate
12 min
Al-Khafi (Hidden Text) and Its Application to Theft Cases
Defines khafi and applies it to two theft cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-khafi in Hanafi usul, identify its cause, and apply it to the cases of the pickpocket (tarrar) and grave robber (nabbash) to derive the ruling on the hadd of cutting.

Core Idea
A ruling is khafi when the text is clear but its application to a specific case is hidden due to an external factor like a distinct name.

You have learned about zahir (manifest) texts, but not all clear rulings apply straightforwardly. Sometimes a general command like 'cut the hand of the thief' appears to cover all thieves, but when we encounter a pickpocket or a grave robber, new doubts arise. In this lesson, we explore the concept of khafi (hidden) as a distinct category in the Hanafi hierarchy of texts, and see how the scholars used ijtihad to resolve whether the hadd applies.

Guided Path
1
Define al-khafi
Understand the definition: a ruling that is apparent in its original context but hidden when applied to specific cases due to external factors like distinct names.
Khafi is not the same as mujmal; it is a type of zahir text that becomes ambiguous in application.
2
Identify the external factor
In the theft verse, the terms 'tarrar' (pickpocket) and 'nabbash' (grave robber) are distinct names in common usage, causing doubt whether they fall under the general term 'thief'.
This external factor creates the hiddenness.
3
Examine the legal meaning of theft
Analyze the definition of theft to see if the pickpocket and grave robber share the full meaning. The pickpocket has an increase in the meaning (stealing from an alert owner), while the grave robber has a decrease (stealing from the dead).
The increase strengthens the analogy; the decrease weakens it.
4
Apply the ruling via dalalat al-nass
Using a fortiori reasoning (dalalat al-nass), the hadd is extended to the pickpocket because the increase in meaning makes them even more deserving of punishment, but not to the grave robber due to the decrease.
This is the Hanafi position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Analyze the dispute
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i consider the text khafi for both but treat them as covered by the general term, leading to cutting for both. The Hanafi response is that the hadith of Ali is carried on siyasa, not as a fixed hadd.
The preferred view is no cutting for the grave robber.

تعريف الخفي وحكمه ظاهر في حق وجوب قطع اليد لكل سارق خفي في حق الطرار والنباش؛ لأنهما اختصا باسم آخر غير السارق في عرف أهل اللسان، فتأملنا فوجدنا أن اختصاص الطرار باسم آخر لأجل زيادة معنى السرقة إذ السرقة هو أخذ مال محترم محرز خفية، وهو يسرق ممن الطرار هو يقظان قاصد لحفظ المال بضرب غفلة وفترة تعتريه واختصاص النباش به لأجل اسم آخر نقصان معنى السرقة فيه؛ لأنه يسرق من الموتى الذي هو غير قاصد للحفظ، فعدينا حكم القطع إلى الطرار لأجل الزيادة فيه بدلالة النص ولم نعد إلى النباش لأجل النقصان فيه، ولو كان القبر في بيت مقفل قيل: لا يقطع النباش؛ لما ذكرنا، وقيل: يقطع؛ لوجود الحرز بالمكان وإن لم يوجد بالحافظ، وهذا كله عندنا، وقال أبو يوسف والشافعي: الحمل من لكل سارق إلخ فحكم السارق خفي في حق الطرار والنباش لعارض من الخارج، وهو اختصاصهما باسم آخر يعرفان به. (السنبلي) لأنهما اختصا إلخ فتطرقت الشبهة في أنه يشملها اسم السارق أم لا، فتأملنا في المعنى الشرعي للسارق فوجدنا إلخ. (القمر) إذ السرقة إلخ : قلت : التعريف الجامع للسرقة: أخذ مال معتبر شرعًا، من حرز أجنبي لا شبهة فيه خفية، وهو قاصد للحفظ، في نومه أو غيبته احترز بالأول ما دون النصاب، وبالثاني عن الأخذ غير الحرز، وبالثالث عن ذي رحم محرم وبالرابع عن مال فيه شبهة الحرز كمال الشركة، وبالخامس عن والانتهاب، وبالسادس عن النبش وبالسابع عن الطرار. (السنبلي) :محترم أي معزز بأن يكون المال متقوما يحل الانتفاع به شرعًا، فلا قطع بسرقة خمر مسلم، وأن يكون عشرة دراهم، فلا قطع بسرقة أقل منها (القمر) محرز واحترز بقوله : محرز عن الأخذ من غير حرز، ويقول: خفية عن الانتهاب والغصب كذا قال ابن الملك. (القمر) بدلالة لأن الحكم إذا ثبت في الأدنى يثبت في الأعلى بالأولى. (المحشي) بدلالة النص متعلق بقوله فعدينا وفيه أن الحد للزجر، وزاجر الأدنى لا يثبت في الأعلى دلالة، ألا ترى أن الكفارة في قتل الخطاء لا تثبت في قتل العمد دلالة على أن الزاجر مشروع فيما كثر وقوعه، فلا يلزم شرعه فيما قل وقوعه كالطر، فإنه أقل وقوعا من السرقة، ولذا قال بعض شراح أصول البزدوي: إن إثبات القطع في الطرار بالعبارة؛ لأن المطلق يتناول الكامل فلأن يتناول الأكمل أولى. (القمر) قيل: لا يقطع إلخ: هو الأصح كذا في "الدر المختار" وهو قول الإمام السرخسي كذا قال البرجندي. (القمر) لما ذكرنا أي لأجل النقصان في اللفظ، وكل من الناس يتأول في الدخول في ذلك البيت لزيارة القبر. (القمر) : وهذا أي عدم قطع النباش عند الإمام الأعظم وعند محمد رحمها. (القمر) يقطع النباش على كل حال؛ لقوله علي: من نبش قطعناه ، قلنا: هو محمول على السياسة؛ لما روي عنه علي: "لا قطع على المختفي"، وهو النباش بلغة أهل المدينة.
Definition of the hidden (khafi) and its ruling: apparent in terms of the obligation of cutting off the hand for every thief, hidden in the case of the pickpocket (tarrar) and the grave robber (nabbash); because they are distinguished by another name besides 'thief' in the custom of the people of the language. So we reflected and found that the pickpocket's distinction by another name is due to an increase in the meaning of theft—since theft is taking protected wealth from a secure place secretly—and he steals from one who is awake and intending to guard the wealth, but he strikes him with heedlessness and a momentary lapse. The grave robber's distinction by another name is due to a decrease in the meaning of theft in him; because he steals from the dead who are not intending to guard. So we extended the ruling of cutting to the pickpocket due to the increase in him by indication of the text, and we did not extend it to the grave robber due to the decrease in him. If the grave is in a locked house, it is said: the grave robber is not cut; for what we mentioned. And it is said: he is cut due to the existence of secure enclosure by place even if not by guardian. And all this is according to us. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i said: the general meaning from 'every thief' etc., so the ruling of the thief is hidden in the case of the pickpocket and grave robber due to an external factor, which is their distinction by another name by which they are known. (al-Sanbali) Because they are distinguished etc., so doubt arose whether the name 'thief' includes them or not, so we reflected on the legal meaning of thief and found etc. (al-Qamar) 'For theft' etc. I said: the comprehensive definition of theft: taking wealth considered legally protected, from a secure enclosure belonging to a stranger, without any legally recognized right, secretly, while the owner intends to guard it, whether asleep or absent. Caution: by the first, less than the nisab; by the second, taking from other than secure enclosure; by the third, from a mahram relative; by the fourth, wealth with doubtful secure enclosure like partnership property; by the fifth, from plunder; by the sixth, from grave robbing; by the seventh, from pickpocketing. (al-Sanbali) 'Protected' meaning valued, such that the wealth is legally beneficial and permissible to use; so there is no cutting for theft of a Muslim's wine, and it must be ten dirhams, so no cutting for theft of less than that. (al-Qamar) 'Secure enclosure' and he cautions by his saying 'secure enclosure' against taking from other than secure enclosure, and he says 'secretly' against plunder and usurpation; such is said by Ibn al-Malik. (al-Qamar) 'By indication' because if the ruling is established for the lower, it is established for the higher a fortiori. (al-Muhashi) 'By indication of the text' is attached to his saying 'we extended'; and therein that the hadd is for deterrence, and the deterrence of the lower is not established for the higher by indication. Do you not see that the expiation for accidental killing is not established for intentional killing by indication? This shows that deterrence is legislated for that which frequently occurs, so its legislation is not required for that which rarely occurs, like pickpocketing, for it occurs less often than theft. Hence some commentators of Usul al-Bazdawi said: the establishment of cutting for the pickpocket is by explicit wording; because the absolute encompasses the complete, so encompassing the more complete is more appropriate. (al-Qamar) 'It is said: not cut etc.' is the correct opinion as in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, and it is the statement of Imam al-Sarakhsi, as said by al-Birjandi. (al-Qamar) 'For what we mentioned' meaning due to the decrease in meaning, and every person interprets entering that house for visiting the grave. (al-Qamar) 'This' meaning the non-cutting of the grave robber according to the Great Imam and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them. (al-Qamar) The grave robber is cut in all cases due to the statement of Ali: 'Whoever robs a grave, we cut him.' We say: it is carried on policy (siyasa) due to what is narrated from Ali: 'No cutting for the secret taker (mukhtafi),' which is the grave robber in the dialect of the people of Medina.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition of 'khafi' (hidden text) with practical application. The author defines khafi as a ruling that is apparent in its original context (like the verse on cutting the thief's hand) but becomes hidden when applied to specific cases due to external factors. Here, the cases are the pickpocket (tarrar) and the grave robber (nabbash), who are distinguished by separate names in common usage, creating doubt whether they fall under the general term 'thief'. The author then examines the legal meaning of theft and finds that pickpocketing involves an increase in the meaning (stealing from an alert owner by trickery), while grave robbing involves a decrease (stealing from the dead who have no intent to guard). Using indication of the text (dalalat al-nass) and a fortiori reasoning, the Hanafi position is to apply the cutting punishment to the pickpocket but not to the grave robber. If the grave is in a locked house, there is a difference of opinion: some Hanafis say no cut due to the decreased meaning of theft, others say cut due to the secure enclosure of the place. The author notes that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i consider the ruling khafi for both but do not differentiate; they treat both as covered by the general term. The passage then addresses a counter-argument: the hadith from Ali 'whoever robs a grave, cut him' is explained as a matter of policy (siyasa), not a fixed hadd punishment, and the authentic narration 'no cutting for the mukhtafi' (grave robber) supports the Hanafi view. The commentary concludes with the preferred Hanafi ruling: the grave robber is not cut.

Key Terms
الخفي (al-khafi): A ruling that is apparent in its original context but becomes hidden when applied to specific cases due to external factors like distinct names or nuanced meanings.
الطرار (al-tarrar): A pickpocket who steals from a person who is awake and intending to guard but is momentarily heedless.
النباش (al-nabbash): A grave robber who steals from the dead, who is not intending to guard the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position outlined in this passage, why is the grave robber (nabbash) not subject to the hadd of cutting the hand?
A) Because the grave is not considered a secure enclosure
B) Because the grave robber steals from the dead, who do not intend to guard, thus the meaning of theft is diminished
C) Because the grave robber's name is not included in the general term 'thief'
D) Because Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i disagreed with the cutting

Key Takeaway
The category of khafi requires the mujtahid to examine the effective cause and the presence of any external factor that may exclude the specific case from the general ruling.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy

Definition and Ruling of Mushkil (Problematic) Text
Defines mushkil and applies to grave robber

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mushkil text, state its ruling in Hanafi usul, and identify when a text qualifies as mushkil using the grave robber example.

Core Idea
Mushkil text is ambiguous due to multiple similar meanings; we must seek and reflect to uncover the Lawgiver's intent.

Not all unclear texts are equal. In Hanafi usul, mushkil occupies a distinct level of ambiguity: it is clearer than mujmal but less clear than khafi. This lesson unpacks its definition, ruling, and a practical application—the controversial hadith on cutting the hand of a grave robber. By mastering this concept, you will understand how the Hanafi school determines when a text requires ijtihad and when it can be acted upon directly.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the central metaphor
The author compares mushkil to a stranger who changes his clothes to blend in. Recognize that mushkil means the text 'enters into its forms' (ashkal)—it resembles other meanings, making it hard to single out the intended one.
Think of a word like 'ayn that can mean eye, spring, or spy.
2
Locate mushkil in the clarity hierarchy
Mushkil is placed above khafi (hidden) and below mujmal (ambiguous requiring bayan). Understand that it has 'an increase in hiddenness over the hidden.'
Compare with nass (explicit) which increases clarity over zahir (apparent).
3
Identify the ruling (hukm) of mushkil
The passage states: believe the text is truth from the Lawgiver, then engage in seeking (talab) and reflection (ta'ammul) until the intended meaning becomes clear.
This ruling means you cannot simply reject or ignore a mushkil text; you must strive to understand it.
4
Apply to the grave robber case
The hadith 'man nabasha qata'nahu' is evaluated. Scholars dispute its authenticity and meaning. Some accept it as proof for cutting, others (Ibn Abbas) reject it. The passage concludes the hadith is munkar and the cutting punishment does not apply.
This shows how mushkil operates in practice: the text's ambiguity and weak transmission require ijtihad, and the stronger evidence prevails.

It is like a stranger who mingles with other people by changing his clothes and appearance, so it has an increase in hiddenness over the hidden. It corresponds to the explicit (nass) which has an increase in clarity over the apparent. For this reason it requires two considerations: seeking and then reflecting, as he said. [6/531, chapter on what has been said about the grave robber's punishment]

Commentary
This passage provides the usul definition of 'mushkil' (problematic text). A mushkil text is one that is ambiguous because it resembles other meanings; it is like a stranger who changes his appearance to blend in. It has an even greater degree of hiddenness than khafi. The ruling for mushkil: one must firmly believe that the text is truth and then engage in rigorous seeking and reflection until the intended meaning of the Lawgiver becomes clear. The passage then applies this to the grave robber case: the hadith 'whoever robs a grave, cut him' is considered mushkil because its authenticity is disputed and its meaning is uncertain. The author cites various scholars who deem the hadith weak or munkar, and contrasts it with the narration from Ibn 'Abbas that the grave robber is not cut. The commentator (al-Qamar) further explains the etymology: mushkil is derived from 'ashkala' meaning 'entered into its forms' (ashkal), i.e., the text has multiple possible meanings and thus becomes hidden. The passage concludes with references to hadith criticism, showing that the reliable position is that the grave robber is not subject to the fixed cutting punishment.

Key Terms
المشكل (al-mushkil): A textual form that has multiple similar meanings (ashkal), requiring reflection to determine the intended one.

Mutashabih: Definition, Ruling, and the Ta'wil Dispute
Defines mutashabih and presents the Hanafi argument for the limitation of knowing its ta'wil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mutashabih, state its ruling, and apply the Hanafi argument from the Quranic verse to justify why scholars do not know its ta'wil.

Core Idea
Mutashabih: a text whose intended meaning is permanently unknown; we believe it is true but only Allah knows its ta'wil.

This passage defines mutashabih as an inherently obscure category of Quranic verses, establishing that its ta'wil (true interpretation) is known only to Allah. The author then contrasts the Hanafi view with that of al-Shafi'i and the Mu'tazila, grounding the difference in the recitation (waqf) of Aal Imran 7. Understanding this definition and the proof behind it is essential for correctly handling ambiguous texts in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
1. Grasp the definition of mutashabih
Identify how the passage defines mutashabih: a text whose intended meaning is permanently inaccessible, unlike muhkam (clear) or mujmal (which can become clear). Note the analogy of a lost man whose trace is cut off.
Mutashabih is the third category after muhkam and mujmal in the Hanafi hierarchy.
2
2. Understand the ruling (i'tiqad ijmail)
Extract the ruling: one must believe that the mutashabih text conveys truth (i'tiqad al-haqqiyah) without knowing its specific meaning. This is called i'tiqad ijmail (total belief). After the Resurrection, it will become clear.
The Prophet (peace be upon him) knew the meaning during his life, otherwise communication would be futile.
3
3. Identify the Hanafi proof from the Quran
Locate the verse (Aal Imran 7) and the Hanafi argument: the word 'wa' in 'wa al-rasikhun' begins a new sentence because one must pause after 'except Allah'. This makes 'al-rasikhun' the subject of a new sentence saying 'we believe', not 'they know'.
The grammatical rule of waqf (pause) in Quranic recitation is the key evidence.
4
4. Contrast with the opposing view
Understand that al-Shafi'i and the Mu'tazila read the verse continuously, making 'wa al-rasikhun' conjuncted to 'Allah', so they infer that the rasikhun also know ta'wil.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is the permissibility of ta'wil by scholars: Hanafis restrict it to methodological interpretation within rules, others allow deeper speculative ta'wil.
5
5. Apply the Hanafi rule to a new case
Given a mutashabih verse (e.g., about divine attributes), you should now be able to state that the correct stance is i'tiqad ijmail without attempting to know the ta'wil, and cite the pause argument as justification.
This prepares you for actual fatwa and tafsir work within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف المتشابه وحكمه وأما المتشابه فهو اسم لما انقطع رجاء معرفة المراد منه، ولا يرجى بدوه أصلاً، فهو في غاية الخفاء بمنزلة المحكم في غاية الظهور، فصار كرجل مفقود عن بلده وانقطع أثره، وانقضى أقرانه وجيرانه. أي مات وحكمه اعتقاد الحقية قبل يوم الإصابة أي اعتقاد أن المراد به حق وإن لم نعلمه قبل يوم القيامة، وأما بعد القيامة فيصير مكشوفا لكل أحد إن شاء الله تعالى، وهذا في حق الأمة، وأما في حق النبي عليه السلام، فكان معلومًا وإلا تبطل فائدة التخاطب، ويصير التخاطب بالنبي بالمهمل كالتكلم بالزنجي مع العربي، وهذا عندنا، وقال الشافعي رحمه الله وعامة المعتزلة: إن العلماء الراسخين أيضًا يعلمون تأويله. ومنشأ الخلاف: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا يَعْلَمُ تَأْوِيلَهُ إِلَّا اللَّهُ وَالرَّاسِحُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ يَقُولُونَ آمَنَّا بِهِ﴾ (آل عمران:۷) فعندنا يجب الوقف على قوله إلا الله وقوله وَالرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ جملة مبتدأة؛ (القمر) ولا يرجى بدوه أصلاً: سواء كان عدم رجاء بدو المراد عارضيا كالمجمل الذي توفي النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بلا بيانه، أو ذاتيا بأن يعرف بالنقل من الرسول انقطاع رجاء بدو المراد مع تردد العقل فيه أيضًا، أو لأنه مما لا يقدر على فهمه كمسألة القدر كذا قيل. (القمر) أي اعتقاد أن المراد إلخ المراد بالاعتقاد الاعتقاد الإجمالي، فإنه يكون قبل الإصابة إلى المراد، وأما بعد الإصابة إلى المراد فيكون الاعتقاد تفصيلاً فاحفظه. (القمر) بالزنجي مع العربي: أي باللسان الزنجي أي لسان الحبش مع الرجل العربي. (القمر) وهذا: أي انقطاع رجاء معرفة المراد من المتشابه. (القمر) جملة مبتدأة وليس معطوف على الله؛ لأن الوقف على المعطوف عليه قبل ذكر المعطوف في موضع الاشتباه ممتنع عند القراء كذا قال بحر العلوم أي مولانا عبد العلي له. (القمر)
Definition of the mutashabih and its ruling. As for mutashabih, it is a name for that from which hope of knowing its intended meaning has been cut off, and its manifestation is not hoped for at all. It is in extreme hiddenness, like the muhkam is in extreme clarity. It became like a man lost from his country, his trace cut off, and his peers and neighbors have passed away—i.e., he died. Its ruling is to believe in its truth before the Day of manifestation, i.e., to believe that its intended meaning is true even if we do not know it before the Day of Resurrection. As for after the Resurrection, it will become disclosed to everyone, Allah willing. This is regarding the community. As for regarding the Prophet (peace be upon him), it was known, otherwise the purpose of communication would be nullified, and speaking to the Prophet would be like speaking in the Ethiopian language with an Arab—i.e., meaningless. This is our view. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) and most of the Mu'tazila said that the firmly rooted scholars (rasikhun) also know its ta'wil. The origin of the disagreement is His statement: 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge say: we believe in it' (Aal Imran 7). According to us, one must stop at His statement 'except Allah', and the phrase 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge' is an independent sentence (mubtada'). (Al-Qamar) And its manifestation is not hoped for at all: whether the lack of hope for the intended meaning is accidental, like the mujmal that the Prophet died without clarifying, or inherent, such that it is known by transmission from the Messenger that hope for its manifestation is cut off, and the intellect is also perplexed about it, or because it is something that cannot be understood, like the issue of qadar (divine decree), as was said. (Al-Qamar) i.e., to believe that the intended meaning etc., the meaning of belief is total belief (i'tiqad ijmail), for it occurs before reaching the intended meaning. As for after reaching the intended meaning, the belief becomes detailed, so preserve this. (Al-Qamar) In the Ethiopian language with an Arab: i.e., in the Ethiopian language, the language of Abyssinia, with the Arab man. (Al-Qamar) And this: i.e., the cutting off of hope of knowing the intended meaning from mutashabih. (Al-Qamar) An independent sentence and not conjuncted to 'Allah' because stopping on the conjuncted item before mentioning the conjunctive in a place of ambiguity is impermissible among reciters, as said by Bahr al-Ulum, i.e., our master 'Abd al-'Ali. (Al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of mutashabih (ambiguous/unclear) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Mutashabih is that text or matter from which all hope of understanding the intended meaning is lost; it will never become clear in this world, as opposed to muhkam which is extremely clear. The author likens it to a missing person whose trace and contemporaries are gone—essentially dead. Its ruling is that we must believe it is true (i.e., that it conveys a true meaning from Allah) even without knowing that meaning, and after the Resurrection its reality will become manifest. The Prophet (peace be upon him) knew the meaning of mutashabih, otherwise communication would be pointless. The Hanafi position (followed by the author) is that only Allah knows the ta'wil (interpretation) of mutashabih, while the scholars firmly rooted in knowledge merely say 'we believe in it'—they do not know its specific meaning. This is based on the verse from Aal Imran. The proof lies in the waqf (pause) in recitation: one must stop at 'except Allah', making 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge' the beginning of a new sentence, thus separating them from the knowledge of ta'wil. Al-Shafi'i and the Mu'tazila argue that the rasikhun also know the ta'wil by reading the verse continuously. The passage then further explains that lack of hope for manifestation can be accidental (like a mujmal that was never clarified) or inherent (like matters of divine decree that intellect cannot grasp). This is a key point of usul disagreement between Hanafis and others.

Key Terms
المتشابه (al-mutashabih): That whose intended meaning cannot be known, and hope for its manifestation is cut off.
التأويل (al-ta'wil): The true interpretation or intended meaning of a text; for mutashabih, only Allah knows it.
الراسخون في العلم (al-rasikhun fi al-ilm): Scholars firmly grounded in knowledge; their statement is 'we believe in it', not a claim to know ta'wil.
الاعتقاد الإجمالي (al-i'tiqad al-ijmail): Total belief in the truth of a matter without knowing its details.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how does the verse 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge say: we believe in it' support the Hanafi view that scholars do not know the ta'wil of mutashabih?
A) The verse clearly states that only Allah knows ta'wil, and scholars are excluded.
B) The verse requires a pause after 'except Allah' because the phrase 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge' is a new sentence expressing faith, not knowledge.
C) The verse was abrogated by other texts.
D) The verse uses the word 'ya'lamu' (knows) only for Allah, not for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Mutashabih texts demand total belief without detailed knowledge; the Quranic pause rule is the Hanafi argument for why scholars do not know their ta'wil.

Hanafi Text Clarity Hierarchy

Haqiqah Does Not Drop and the Priority of Literal Meaning
States two principles and gives examples and a Quranic application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi principles regarding haqiqah and majaz, apply them to derive rulings from a Quranic verse, and identify the epistemological priority of literal meaning.

Core Idea
Haqiqah always applies to its subject; majaz is only used when literal meaning is impossible.

This lesson introduces two foundational principles of Hanafi usul al-fiqh: that the literal meaning (haqiqah) never drops from its true referent, and that whenever the literal meaning can be applied, the metaphorical meaning (majaz) is abandoned. These principles guide how we interpret words in the Quran and hadith and have direct fiqhi consequences, as shown in the example of oath-making. We will work through the passage to understand the reasoning and see how it shapes Hanafi rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two principles in the passage
Read the opening sentences. The first principle states that haqiqah never drops from the named object. The second states that when acting upon the literal meaning is possible, the metaphorical drops. Note that these are given as 'alama (signs) to distinguish haqiqah from majaz.
The Arabic phrases are: 'الْحَقِيقَةُ لَا تَسْقُطُ عَنِ الْمُسَمَّى' and 'مَتَى أَمْكَنَ الْعَمَلُ بِهَا سَقَطَ الْمَجَازُ'.
2
Understand the test: negation of haqiqah vs. majaz
The passage explains that for haqiqah, you cannot negate the term from its subject (e.g., you cannot say 'he is not a father' for a biological father). For majaz, you can both affirm and negate it (e.g., a grandfather can be called 'father' metaphorically but it is not really a father). This is the test to tell literal from metaphorical.
Example: a brave man can be called 'a lion' but it is not a literal lion.
3
Apply the second principle to the Quranic verse
The passage cites Quran 5:89, 'Allah takes you to account for what you have bound your oaths.' The word 'aqd' has three levels: literal (tying a knot), metaphorical (verbal contract), and more metaphorical (intention). Since the literal meaning is possible (the binding of words), the Hanafi interpretation restricts the verse to intentional, bound oaths (al-yamin al-mun'aqida), not careless ones (al-ghamus).
This is the istinbat chain: the word 'aqd' is analyzed, the literal meaning is preferred, and the ruling is derived.
4
Work through the fiqhi application: husband's oath
The passage gives a fiqhi case: if someone swears not to 'marry' a woman who is his wife, the oath applies to sexual intercourse (literal) not the contract (metaphorical). If the woman is a stranger, it applies to the contract because the literal meaning (intercourse with a stranger) is prohibited by shariah and thus abandoned, so the metaphorical meaning takes over.
This shows how the principle plays out: when the literal meaning is blocked by shariah, majaz is used.
5
Note the cross-madhhab issue on generality of majaz
The passage mentions that some Hanafis hold that majaz does not have generality (umum), while the Shafi'i school (according to some) holds that it does. The author notes that the correct view in both schools is that majaz can have generality under certain conditions. This is a point of ikhtilaf that affects rulings on riba.
This step is advanced; focus on the two principles first.

والمراد أن المعنى الحقيقي لا يسقط ولا ينتفي عما صدق عليه، بخلاف المعنى المجازي، فإنه أن يصدق عليه، ويصح أن ينفي عنه، يقال للأب: أب ولا يصح أن يقال: إنه ليس بأب بخلاف الجد، فإنه يصح أن يقال: إنه أب ويصح أن يقال: إنه ليس بأب، وكذا أي مجازا الهيكل المعلوم يصح أن يقال عليه : إنه أسد ولا ينفي عنه بأن يقال: إنه ليس بأسد، بخلاف
الرجل الشجاع، فإنه يصح أن يقال: إنه أسد وأن يقال: إنه ليس بأسد: ومتى أمكن العمل بها سقط المجاز ، هذا أصل كبير لنا يتفرع عليه كثير من الأحكام أي ما دام أمكن العمل

(القمر) لأنه مجاز :إلخ وليس للخصم أن يمنع كون العزم معنى مجازيا للعقد بدلالة استعماله فيه عرفا؛ لأن مداره على الأئمة الواضعين. (القمر)
And the literal meaning does not fall away from the named object, unlike the metaphorical meaning. This is a sign to distinguish literal from metaphorical. ... [The passage then explains that the literal meaning never ceases to apply to what it true of, unlike metaphorical meaning which can be negated. For example, for a father, you cannot say he is not a father, but for a grandfather, you can say he is a father and also not a father. Similarly, for a known structure, you can say it is a lion but also negate it, unlike a brave man. And when it is possible to act upon the literal meaning, the metaphorical meaning drops. This is a great principle from which many rulings branch. For example, the phrase 'Allah takes you to account for what you have bound your oaths' is interpreted literally as the bound (intentional) oath, not the mere resolution, because that is literal and the metaphorical does not conflict with the literal.]
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents two fundamental principles of usul al-fiqh regarding haqiqah (literal meaning) and majaz (metaphorical meaning). First, the literal meaning of a word never drops from its true subject; it always applies to what it actually denotes. For example, the word 'father' always applies to a biological father, but 'grandfather' can be called 'father' metaphorically and can also be negated. Second, whenever it is possible to apply the literal meaning of a word, the metaphorical interpretation is abandoned. This principle is illustrated with the Quranic verse about oaths: the word 'aqd' (binding) is literally used for tying a knot, then metaphorically for a verbal contract, and further metaphorically for intention. The Hanafi ruling is that the verse refers only to intentional oaths (al-yamin al-mun'aqida), not to careless ones (al-ghamus), because the literal meaning (the act of binding) is closer. The commentary provides a fiqhi example: if someone swears not to have sex with his wife, it applies to actual intercourse (literal), not the marriage contract (metaphorical), unless the wife is a stranger then it applies to the contract because the literal meaning is abandoned by shariah.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): The literal meaning of a word, which is the original meaning for which it was coined.
المجاز (al-majāz): The metaphorical meaning of a word, used due to a relationship and a qarina that prevents the literal meaning.
المسمى (al-musammā): The named object, i.e., the actual referent of the word.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi usul principle, if both a literal and a metaphorical interpretation of a word are possible, which one is preferred?
A) The metaphorical interpretation.
B) The literal interpretation.
C) Both are used together.
D) The one that is more common in speech.

Key Takeaway
Literal meaning (haqiqah) always retains its original referent and is preferred over metaphorical meaning (majaz) whenever it can be applied in practice.

Siyaq al-Nazm: Contextual Qarina Overriding Literal Meaning
Introduction to siyaq al-nazm with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a contextual qarina (siyaq al-nazm) in a text and explain how it overrides the literal meaning, using the Hanafi usul principle.

Core Idea
Contextual qarina turns a literal command into a threat or reprimand.

In this lesson, we explore how the flow of speech (siyaq al-nazm) provides a verbal indicator that can override the apparent meaning. Two classic examples—a man telling another 'Divorce my wife if you are a man' and the Quranic verse 'So whoever wills, let him believe…'—demonstrate how contextual clues shift interpretation from literal to metaphorical, reflecting the actual intent of the speaker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text
Read the Arabic statement or verse carefully. Note its apparent grammatical structure.
For the first example, the text is a conditional sentence. For the second, a Quranic imperative.
2
Determine the literal meaning (haqiqah)
Assume the words are used in their primary sense. For 'Divorce my wife if you are a man', the literal meaning is an agency (tawkeel) to divorce. For the verse, 'mashi'ah' implies free choice without consequence.
This is what a beginner would understand without context.
3
Locate the qarina (verbal indicator)
Find the phrase that contradicts or qualifies the literal meaning. In the marriage example, it is 'if you are a man'—a phrase typically used to challenge ability. In the verse, it is the immediate threat 'Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire.'
The qarina may precede or follow the main statement.
4
Explain why the qarina overrides
The qarina shows that the literal meaning cannot be the intended one. For the marriage example, the condition 'if you are a man' implies doubt about the addressee's manliness, so the statement is actually a reprimand. For the verse, the threat of punishment contradicts the idea of consequence-free choice, so the verse is a warning.
The overriding is based on the impossibility of the literal meaning coexisting with the qarina.
5
State the final interpretation
Conclude that the speech is metaphorical (majaz): the marriage statement is taubeeh (reprimand), and the verse is tahdid (threat).
This final meaning is taken for legal derivation (istinbat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبدلالة سياق النظم أي بسبب سوق الكلام بقرينة لفظية التحقت به سواء كانت سابقة أو متأخرة. كقوله: طلق امرأتي إن كنت رجلاً حتى لا يكون توكيلاً، فإن حقيقة هذا الكلام هو أي طلق امرأتي التوكيل بالطلاق لكن ترك ذلك بقرينة قوله : 'إن كنت رجلاً؛ لأن هذا الكلام إنما يقال: أي إن كنت رجلاً عند إرادة إظهار عجز المخاطب عن الفعل الذي قرن به ذلك الفعل فيكون الكلام للتوبيخ مجازا. ومثله قوله تعالى: فَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيُؤْ مِنْ وَمَنْ شَاءَ فَلْيَكْفُرْ إِنَّا أَعْتَدْنَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ نَاراً حيث تركت حقيقة المشيئة رفع الإثم، وقرينة السياق لا تناسبه، فإنها حاكمة بتحقق الإثم للظالمين أي الكافرين.
And by the indication of the context of the speech (siyaq al-nazm) – i.e., due to the flow of speech with a verbal qarina attached to it, whether preceding or following. Like his statement: 'Divorce my wife if you are a man' – so it is not an agency. The literal meaning of this speech is agency for divorce, but it is abandoned due to the qarina of his statement 'if you are a man'; because this statement is only said when intending to show the addressee's inability to do the action connected to it, so the speech is metaphorical for reprimand. And similarly the statement of Allah, the Exalted: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve. Indeed, We have prepared for the wrongdoers a fire.' (Quran 18:29) – where the literal meaning of will (mashi'ah) – raising sin – is abandoned, and the contextual qarina does not suit it, for it rules the realization of sin for the wrongdoers, i.e., the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage introduces the third type of qarina: siyaq al-nazm (context of speech). A verbal qarina – either before or after the statement – can indicate that the literal meaning is not intended. The first example: a person says to another 'Divorce my wife if you are a man.' Literally, this is an agency (tawkeel) to divorce, but the qarina 'if you are a man' shows it is actually a reprimand implying 'you are not a man to do that.' So the statement is metaphorical for taubeeh (reprimand). The second example from Quran 18:29: 'So whoever wills, let him believe, and whoever wills, let him disbelieve.' The literal meaning of 'will' (mashi'ah) would mean no sin or reward (raf' al-ithm), but the context – the threat of punishment for wrongdoers – shows it is actually a warning and implying that disbelief leads to punishment. Hence the qarina overrides the literal meaning. This demonstrates how usul experts use contextual indicators to determine the intended meaning.

Key Terms
سياق النظم (siyaq al-nazm): The contextual flow of speech that includes a verbal qarina (preceding or following) indicating a non-literal meaning.
قرينة لفظية (qarina lafziyyah): A verbal indicator in the text that modifies the apparent meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It makes the divorce conditional.
B) It indicates the speaker is unsure.
C) It shows the addressee's inability, turning the statement into a reprimand.
D) It is a legal condition for divorce.

Key Takeaway
Siyaq al-nazm uses a verbal qarina to override literal meaning, revealing the actual intent behind a command or permission.

The Meaning of Fa: Connection and Immediate Succession as a Single Concept
Defines the conjunction fa as indicating connection and immediate succession as a single meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi usul definition of the conjunction fa as indicating connection and immediate succession, and apply it to interpret conditional statements in divorce.

Core Idea
Fa indicates a single meaning: connection with immediate succession, not two separate meanings.

This passage from Nur al-Anwar defines the Arabic letter fa, which is crucial in Hanafi usul for interpreting conditional clauses. The author distinguishes fa from 'thumma' and clarifies that the delay implied by fa is minimal and not a waiting period. Through a divorce example, the passage shows how this definition affects legal conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of fa
Locate where the passage states that fa is for connection (waṣl) and immediate succession (taʿqīb). Note that these are not two separate meanings but one composite meaning.
The commentator explicitly says 'the whole is one meaning'.
2
Distinguish fa from thumma
Find where the passage explains that the delay in fa is not the same as the delay implied by thumma (which implies a waiting period). The term 'delay' (tarākhī) is used linguistically, not technically.
The technical delay with muhlah is reserved for thumma.
3
Analyze the conditional divorce example
Read the example: 'If you enter this house, then this house (fa), then you are divorced.' Determine what constitutes fulfillment of the condition based on the definition of fa.
The second entry must follow immediately after the first without any perceptible delay.
4
Consider the glosses on 'laṭufa' (subtle)
The glosses clarify that 'laṭufa' refers to the minuteness of the time interval, not its quality. The delay is necessary but imperceptible.
Even a subtle time gap is allowed, but no intentional waiting.
5
Synthesize the legal effect
Understand that the use of fa in a conditional statement imposes a strict requirement of immediate succession. If the condition is not met (e.g., reversed order, delay), the conditional consequence does not occur.
This affects rulings in divorce, contracts, and other areas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أقسام التقسيم الثالث بحث الفاء] ٣٣٥ بحث الفاء والفاء للوصل والتعقيب أي لكون المعطوف موصولاً بالمعطوف عليه متعقبا له بلا مهلة، فيتراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه بزمان وإن لطف أي قل ذلك الزمان بحيث لا يدرك؛ أي فيتأخر إذ لو لم يكن الزمان فاصلاً أصلاً كان مقارنا تستعمل فيه كلمة "مع"، وإطلاق التراخي ههنا بالمعنى اللغوي لا الاصطلاحي الذي كان مدلول "ثم". أي التأخير بمهلة فإذا قال: "إن دخلت هذه الدار فهذه الدار فأنت طالق فالشرط أن تدخل الثانية بعد الأولى بلا تراخ، فإن لم تدخل الدارين أو دخلت إحداهما فقط، أو دخلت الأولى بعد الثانية، أو دخلت الثانية بعد الأولى بتراخ لم تطلق؛ لأنه لم يوجد الشرط. أي لكون إلخ لما كان يفهم من ظاهر كلام المصنف أن الفاء موضوعة للمعنيين أي الوصل والتعقيب وليس كذلك أجاب عنه بعض الشارحين بأن الواو بمعنى "مع ، والمعنى أن "الفاء" موضوعة للوصل مع التعقيب، وإليه يشير الشارح بقوله: أي لكون إلخ. (القمر) أي لكون المعطوف إلخ فلا يرد أنه يفهم من عبارة الماتن أن للفاء معنيين: أحدهما: الوصل، والثاني: التعقيب، وليس كذلك، بل المجموع معنى واحد فافهم. (السنبلي) وإن لطف قال بحر العلوم أي مولانا عبد العلي له: إن هذه العبارة توهم أن تراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف : عليه بزمان كثير أيضًا مدلول "الفاء"، فإن معنى العبارة إن لم يلطف ذلك الزمان، وإن لطف مع أنه ليس كذلك فحق العبارة أن يقول: فيتراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه بزمان مع الوصل ولك أن تقول: إن معنى عبارة المصنف أن تراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه بزمان ضروري في الفاء وإن كان ضروريا أن يكون ذلك الزمان لطيفًا قليلاً فتدبر. (القمر) وإن لطف إلخ لما كان يرد عليه أن المفهوم من لفظ لطف كون الزمان عمدة لا فضلة ولا معنى له ههنا كما هو بين فأجاب بأن المراد منه ههنا قلة الزمان لا أفضليته والله أعلم.(السنبلي) أي قل: تفسير لقوله لطف (القمر) فيه أي في مقارنة المعطوف مع المعطوف عليه. (القمر) وإطلاق إلخ: دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن تراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه إنما هو مدلول "ثم" لا مدلول الفاء، فلم قال المصنف: فتراخي المعطوف عن المعطوف عليه؟ (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the third division: the discussion of fa. 335. The discussion of fa. And fa is for connection and immediate succession, i.e., that the conjuncted is connected to the conjuncted-to, following it without delay, so the conjuncted is delayed from the conjuncted-to by a time, even if subtle, i.e., that time is small such that it is not perceived; i.e., it is delayed, because if there were no separating time at all, it would be simultaneous, for which the word 'maʿa' (with) would be used. And the use of 'delay' here is in the linguistic sense, not the technical sense which is the meaning of 'thumma' (then), i.e., delay with a waiting period. So if he says: 'If you enter this house, then this house, then you are divorced', the condition is that you enter the second after the first without delay. If you do not enter both houses, or you enter only one, or you enter the first after the second, or you enter the second after the first with delay, she is not divorced, because the condition has not been realized. i.e., 'for being' etc. Because it might be understood from the apparent wording of the author (of the matn) that fa is assigned to two meanings: connection and immediate succession, but it is not so. Some commentators answered that the waw is in the meaning of 'maʿa' (with), and the meaning is that 'fa' is assigned to connection together with immediate succession, and the commentator (shāriḥ) indicates this by his saying 'i.e., for being' etc. (al-Qamar) i.e., 'that the conjuncted' etc. So it is not objected that from the expression of the author of the matn it is understood that fa has two meanings: one is connection and the second is immediate succession, and it is not so, but the whole is one meaning, so understand. (al-Sanbalī) 'Even if subtle' — said Baḥr al-ʿUlūm (i.e., our master ʿAbd al-ʿAlī) about it: This expression might suggest that the delay of the conjuncted from the conjuncted-to by a large time is also the meaning of 'fa', because the meaning of the expression is if that time is not subtle, and if it is subtle, while it is not so; the correct expression would be: 'so the conjuncted is delayed from the conjuncted-to by a time with connection.' But you can say: The meaning of the author's expression is that the delay of the conjuncted from the conjuncted-to by a time is necessary in fa, even if it is necessary that that time be subtle and small, so reflect. (al-Qamar) 'Even if subtle' etc. Because it might be objected that what is understood from the word 'subtle' is that the time is important not incidental, and it has no meaning here as is clear; so he answered that what is meant here is the smallness of time, not its excellence, and Allah knows best. (al-Sanbalī) i.e., 'small' is an interpretation of his word 'subtle'. (al-Qamar) 'in it' i.e., in the comparison of the conjuncted with the conjuncted-to. (al-Qamar) 'And the use' etc.: Repelling a potential objection, its presentation being that the delay of the conjuncted from the conjuncted-to is only the meaning of 'thumma', not the meaning of fa, so why did the author say: 'so the conjuncted is delayed from the conjuncted-to'? (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the meaning of the Arabic conjunction 'fa'. The author of the matn defines it as indicating both connection (waṣl) and immediate succession (taʿqīb). The commentator clarifies that these are not two separate meanings but a single composite meaning: the conjuncted item follows the first without any appreciable delay, even if the intervening time is extremely brief. The technical term 'delay' (tarākhī) is used here in its linguistic sense, not the technical sense reserved for 'thumma' (which implies a noticeable lapse). The example given is a conditional divorce statement: 'If you enter this house, then this house (fa hādhihi al-dār), then you are divorced.' The condition requires entering the second house immediately after the first; if there is any delay, or if the houses are entered in the wrong order, the condition is not fulfilled and no divorce occurs. The commentary then addresses a potential misunderstanding: that fa has two separate meanings. The correct understanding is that fa inherently combines connection with immediate succession. The glosses (al-Qamar, al-Sanbalī) further refine this, clarifying that the word 'laṭufa' (subtle) refers to minuteness of time, not quality, and that the delay is relative to the action, not absolute. A possible objection is raised that delay is the meaning of 'thumma', but the commentary explains that in fa the delay is not with a waiting period (muhlah), so it is different.

Key Terms
الفاء (al-fāʾ): A conjunction that indicates connection (waṣl) and immediate succession (taʿqīb) as a single meaning, not two separate meanings.
التعقيب (al-taʿqīb): Immediate succession; the conjuncted item follows the first without any appreciable delay.
التراخي (al-tarākhī): Delay; in this passage, it is used in the linguistic sense of general delay, but the technical term for a delay with waiting is reserved for 'thumma'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to his wife: 'If you enter this house, then (fa) this house (i.e., another house), then you are divorced.' Which of the following actions would NOT fulfill the condition?
A) She enters the first house and immediately enters the second house without any time gap.
B) She enters the second house first, then immediately enters the first house.
C) She enters the first house, then after a very brief moment (imperceptible delay) enters the second house.
D) She enters the first house, then after a clear delay of a few minutes, enters the second house.

Key Takeaway
The conjunction fa in Hanafi usul is a single meaning word indicating connection with immediate succession, and its use in conditions imposes a strict requirement of no perceptible delay.

The Hanafi Rule of Ghayah (Endpoint) with the Word Ila
Analyzing the Hanafi rule for inclusion/exclusion of the endpoint after 'ila'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for determining whether a ghayah (endpoint) mentioned after 'ila' is included or excluded from a legal ruling, and identify the crucial condition of the ghayah being qa'imah bi nafsiha or not.

Core Idea
Whether 'ila' includes its endpoint depends on whether the endpoint is independent or part of the original scope.

The word 'ila' (to/until) appears frequently in legal texts, but its effect on the endpoint is not uniform. In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi usul analysis that distinguishes between independent endpoints (like walls) and dependent endpoints (like the elbows in wudu). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting commands and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ghayah and its nature
Determine if the endpoint mentioned after 'ila' is an independent entity that exists without the object of the ruling, or if it is dependent on and naturally part of the object.
For example, a wall exists independently of the house; the elbow exists only as part of the hand.
2
Check if the original statement encompasses the ghayah
If the ghayah is not independent, see if the original term (e.g., 'hand') naturally includes it in its scope.
The hand naturally extends to the armpit, so it encompasses the elbow.
3
Apply the inclusion rule
If the ghayah is independent, it is excluded from the ruling (not included). If it is non-independent and encompassed, the mention of the ghayah serves to exclude what is beyond it, so the ghayah itself is included. Otherwise, it is excluded.
Examples: walls excluded, elbows included.
4
Consider special contexts
For absolute statements (e.g., deferred payment terms without indication of perpetuity), the ghayah is not included by consensus, because the absolute does not necessitate perpetuating the ruling.
Example: 'I defer the price to Ramadan' – Ramadan is not included; payment is due before Ramadan.
5
Evaluate counterarguments
Understand Imam Zufar's objection that all ghayahs are excluded, and see why the Hanafi position is preferred: it is supported by Quranic usage and avoids absurdities.
The anecdote about age (between sixty and seventy) illustrates the absurdity of Zufar's blanket exclusion.

Explanation of the divisions of the third division. Unlike 'man shi'ta'... Research on the word 'ila'. 'wa ila' for the termination of the ghayah, i.e., the termination of the distance; the ghayah is called that as a part for the whole, as is said. The one who said that is the author of al-Talwih. Then he explained the rule: in which place the ghayah is included and in which it is not. He said: If the ghayah is qa'imah bi nafsiha (independent), like his saying: 'from this wall to this wall', the two ghayahs are not included in the iqrar (acknowledgment) because the wall is an independent ghayah, i.e., it exists before speech and is not dependent in its existence on the object of the ghayah, so they are not included in the object. We made an exception by our saying: existing before speech. ... Then know that regarding 'ila' there are four schools among Arabic linguists: first: what comes after it is included in the ruling of what comes before it absolutely; second: absolutely not included; third: included if what comes after is of the same genus as what comes before, otherwise not; fourth: inclusion or non-inclusion requires external evidence, and 'ila' indicates neither inclusion nor non-inclusion. The author gave a detailed classification... The two ghayahs: the start and the end, because the ghayah is the boundary of the object, and the boundary is not included in the bounded... Regarding the terms set for debts and the price in his saying: 'I sold this and deferred the price to a month' or 'I rented it to Ramadan' or 'to tomorrow' and the like, all these although they are apparently independent, they occur after speech... As for the inclusion of the Masjid al-Aqsa in the verse 'Glory be to Him who took His servant by night from the Masjid al-Haram to the Masjid al-Aqsa', it is by well-known narrations, not by the text. If it (the ghayah) is not independent, and if the original statement encompasses the ghayah, then its mention is to exclude what is beyond it, so it is included, as in the case of the elbows in the verse 'and your hands to the elbows', for they are not independent, and the beginning of the statement, 'the hands', encompasses them because the hand extends to the armpit, so its mention is to exclude what is beyond them, so they are included by themselves. Thus what Zufar said is invalid: that every ghayah is not included in the object. ... Regarding the terms, know that there is no difference of narration regarding the terms of debts, price, sale, and lease; rather the ghayah is not included by consensus because the beginning of the statement is absolute and the absolute does not necessitate perpetuity such that the ghayah would be to drop what is beyond it. ... As for the entry of the Masjid al-Aqsa... it is a response to an implied question... This is like the elbows because its mention is not to extend the ruling to it; rather the ruling is already extending, so if it is to drop what is beyond it, it remains included under the ruling of the beginning. ... Thus the elbows are included in the ruling of washing. Thus what Zufar said is invalid: a nice anecdote: he debated al-Asma'i with Zufar regarding inclusion of the ghayah, and al-Asma'i said to Zufar: 'What do you say about a man who is asked his age and says: between sixty and seventy? Would he be nine years old?' Zufar was confounded.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the word 'ila' (to/until) and the rules for whether the endpoint (ghayah) is included in the scope of the ruling or not. The author begins by explaining that 'ila' indicates the termination of a distance. He then outlines four linguistic schools on 'ila': absolute inclusion, absolute exclusion, inclusion only if the endpoint is of the same genus, and a neutral position requiring external evidence. The Hanafi usul position, as presented here, adopts a nuanced approach: if the ghayah is 'qa'imah bi nafsiha' (an independent entity existing before speech and not dependent on the object for its existence), then it is not included. Examples: 'from this wall to this wall' – the walls exist independently, so they are not included in the ruling (e.g., an acknowledgment of property does not include the walls). Conversely, if the ghayah is not independent and the original speech already covers it, then the mention of the ghayah serves to exclude what is beyond it, thus the ghayah itself is included. The prime example is the Quranic verse on wudu: 'and your hands to the elbows' (5:6). The hand naturally extends to the armpit, so mentioning the elbows excludes the upper arm while including the elbows themselves. This refutes Zufar's blanket claim that no ghayah is ever included. The passage also addresses special cases: terms in debts (e.g., 'to Ramadan') are not included because the original statement is absolute and does not imply perpetuity, so the ghayah merely marks the end without inclusion. The inclusion of Masjid al-Aqsa in the Isra verse is established by hadith, not by linguistic analysis, because it is an independent ghayah. The anecdote of Zufar and al-Asma'i humorously illustrates the illogical conclusion of Zufar's rigid position: if 'ila' always excludes, then someone saying 'between sixty and seventy' could be nine years old.

Key Terms
إلى (ila): A preposition indicating the endpoint of a range or duration; its legal effect regarding inclusion of the endpoint is context-dependent.
غاية (ghayah): The endpoint or boundary mentioned after 'ila'; its inclusion depends on whether it is qa'imah bi nafsiha (independent) or not.
قائمة بنفسها (qa'imah bi nafsiha): An independent entity that exists before the speech act and does not depend on the object for its existence; such a ghayah is not included in the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the inclusion of the endpoint after 'ila' depends on whether the endpoint is an independent entity or naturally encompassed by the original ruling.
